	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" >Edsger W. Dijkstra</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		<div id="contentSub2"></div>
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Dutch computer scientist</div>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Edsger Wybe Dijkstra</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/aɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">aɪ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">DYKE</span>-strə</i></a>; <small>Dutch:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Dutch" title="Help:IPA/Dutch">[ˈɛtsxər ˈʋibə ˈdɛikstra]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/85/Dijkstra.ogg" class="internal" title="Dijkstra.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; 11 May 1930 – 6 August 2002) was a Dutch <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_programmer" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer programmer">programmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_engineer" title="Software engineer">software engineer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Systems_scientist" class="mw-redirect" title="Systems scientist">systems scientist</a>, science <a href="/wiki/Essayist" class="mw-redirect" title="Essayist">essayist</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">pioneer in computing science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physicist</a> by training, he worked as a <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> (Amsterdam) from 1952 to 1962. A university professor for much of his life, Dijkstra held the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in Computer Sciences at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> from 1984 until his retirement in 1999. He was a professor of mathematics at the <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a> (1962–1984) and a <a href="/wiki/Research_fellow" title="Research fellow">research fellow</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</a> (1973–1984). In 1972, he became the first non-American, non-British, and <a href="/wiki/Continental_European" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental European">continental European</a> winner of the <a href="/wiki/Turing_Award" title="Turing Award">Turing Award</a>.
</p><p>One of the most influential figures of computing science's founding generation,<sup id="cite_ref-Wirth2005_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wirth2005-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wirth2008-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LLamport2002-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_(2015)-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaplante1996_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaplante1996-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaplante2008_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaplante2008-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Dijkstra helped shape the new discipline both as an engineer and a <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">theorist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> His fundamental contributions cover diverse areas of computing science, including <a href="/wiki/Compiler_construction" class="mw-redirect" title="Compiler construction">compiler construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distributed_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed system">distributed systems</a>, sequential and concurrent programming, programming paradigm and methodology, <a href="/wiki/Programming_language_research" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming language research">programming language research</a>, program design, program development, program verification, software engineering principles, graph algorithms, and philosophical foundations of computer programming and computer science. Many of his papers are the source of new research areas. Several concepts and problems that are now standard in computer science were first identified by Dijkstra and/or bear names coined by him.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the mid-1960s <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">computer programming</a> was considered more an art (or a craft) than a scientific discipline. In <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Mills" title="Harlan Mills">Harlan Mills</a>'s words (1986), "programming [before the 1970s] was regarded as a private, puzzle-solving activity of writing computer instructions to work as a program". In the late 1960s, computer programming was in a <a href="/wiki/Software_crisis" title="Software crisis">state of crisis</a>. Dijkstra was one of a small group of academics and industrial programmers who advocated a new programming style to improve the quality of <a href="/wiki/Computer_program" title="Computer program">programs</a>. Dijkstra, who had a background in mathematics and physics, was one of the driving forces behind the acceptance of computer programming as a scientific discipline.<sup id="cite_ref-DijkstraNYT_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraNYT-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He coined the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a>" and during the 1970s this became the new programming orthodoxy.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroyDenert200219_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroyDenert200219-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> As the originator of the structured programming movement (the first remarkable movement in the history of computer programming), his ideas about programming methodology helped lay the foundations for the birth and development of the professional discipline of <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a>, enabling programmers to organize and manage increasingly complex software projects.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Meyer" title="Bertrand Meyer">Bertrand Meyer</a> (2009) noted, "The revolution in views of programming started by Dijkstra's iconoclasm led to a movement known as structured programming, which advocated a systematic, rational approach to program construction. Structured programming is the basis for all that has been done since in <a href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Meyer2009p188_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2009p188-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The academic study of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a> started in the 1960s, with Dijkstra (1965) credited with being the first paper in this field, identifying and solving the <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem.<sup id="cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LLamport2002-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> He was also one of the early pioneers of the research on principles of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a>. His foundational work on <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">concurrency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphores</a>, mutual exclusion, <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> (deadly embrace), finding <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest paths in graphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fault-tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Fault-tolerance">fault-tolerance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a>, among many other contributions comprises many of the pillars upon which the field of distributed computing is built. Shortly before his death in 2002, he received the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">ACM</a> <a href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential-Paper Award</a> in distributed computing for his work on self-stabilization of program computation. This annual award was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra_Prize" title="Dijkstra Prize">Dijkstra Prize</a> (Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing) the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-DijkstraPrize_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraPrize-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> As the prize, sponsored jointly by the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">Association for Computing Machinery</a> (ACM) <a href="/wiki/Symposium_on_Principles_of_Distributed_Computing" title="Symposium on Principles of Distributed Computing">Symposium on Principles of Distributed Computing</a> (PODC) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Association_for_Theoretical_Computer_Science" title="European Association for Theoretical Computer Science">European Association for Theoretical Computer Science</a> (EATCS) <a href="/wiki/International_Symposium_on_Distributed_Computing" title="International Symposium on Distributed Computing">International Symposium on Distributed Computing</a> (DISC), recognizes that "No other individual has had a larger influence on research in principles of distributed computing".
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Edsger W. Dijkstra was born in <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>.  His father was a chemist who was president of the Dutch Chemical Society; he taught chemistry at a secondary school and was later its superintendent.  His mother was a mathematician, but never had a formal job.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacTutor.bio_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor.bio-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dijkstra had considered a career in law and had hoped to represent the Netherlands in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>. However, after graduating from school in 1948, at his parents' suggestion he studied mathematics and physics and then <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leiden" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Leiden">University of Leiden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Electronic_computers" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic computers">electronic computers</a> were a novelty. Dijkstra stumbled on his career quite by accident, and through his supervisor, Professor A. Haantjes, he met <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_van_Wijngaarden" title="Adriaan van Wijngaarden">Adriaan van Wijngaarden</a>, the director of the Computation Department at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a>, who offered Dijkstra a job; he officially became the Netherlands' first "programmer" in March 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For some time Dijkstra remained committed to physics, working on it in <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a> three days out of each week. With increasing exposure to computing, however, his focus began to shift. As he recalled:<sup id="cite_ref-ewd340_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>After having programmed for some three years, I had a discussion with <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_van_Wijngaarden" title="Adriaan van Wijngaarden">A. van Wijngaarden</a>, who was then my boss at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a>, a discussion for which I shall remain grateful to him as long as I live. The point was that I was supposed to study theoretical physics at the University of Leiden simultaneously, and as I found the two activities harder and harder to combine, I had to make up my mind, either to stop programming and become a real, respectable <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physicist" class="mw-redirect" title="Theoretical physicist">theoretical physicist</a>, or to carry my study of physics to a formal completion only, with a minimum of effort, and to become....., yes what? A <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a>? But was that a respectable profession? For after all, what was <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">programming</a>? Where was the sound body of knowledge that could support it as an intellectually respectable discipline? I remember quite vividly how I envied my hardware colleagues, who, when asked about their professional competence, could at least point out that they knew everything about vacuum tubes, amplifiers and the rest, whereas I felt that, when faced with that question, I would stand empty-handed. Full of misgivings I knocked on Van Wijngaarden's office door, asking him whether I could "speak to him for a moment"; when I left his office a number of hours later, I was another person. For after having listened to my problems patiently, he agreed that up till that moment there was not much of a programming discipline, but then he went on to explain quietly that automatic computers were here to stay, that we were just at the beginning and could not I be one of the persons called to make programming a respectable discipline in the years to come? This was a turning point in my life and I completed my study of physics formally as quickly as I could.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Edsger Dijkstra, The Humble Programmer (EWD340), <a href="/wiki/Communications_of_the_ACM" title="Communications of the ACM">Communications of the ACM</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>When Dijkstra married Maria (Ria) C. Debets in 1957, he was required as a part of the marriage rites to state his profession. He stated that he was a programmer, which was unacceptable to the authorities, there being no such profession then in The Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-ewd340_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1959, he received his PhD from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Amsterdam" title="University of Amsterdam">University of Amsterdam</a> for a thesis entitled 'Communication with an Automatic Computer', devoted to a description of the <a href="/wiki/Assembly_language" title="Assembly language">assembly language</a> designed for the first commercial computer developed in the Netherlands, the X1. His thesis supervisor was Van Wijngaarden.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mathematisch_Centrum.2C_Amsterdam"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematisch_Centrum,_Amsterdam">Mathematisch Centrum, Amsterdam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mathematisch Centrum, Amsterdam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From 1952 until 1962, Dijkstra worked at the <a href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> in Amsterdam,<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> where he worked closely with <a href="/wiki/Bram_Jan_Loopstra" title="Bram Jan Loopstra">Bram Jan Loopstra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carel_S._Scholten" title="Carel S. Scholten">Carel S. Scholten</a>, who had been hired to build a computer. Their mode of interaction was disciplined: They would first decide upon the interface between the hardware and the software, by writing a programming manual. Then the hardware designers would have to be faithful to their part of the contract, while Dijkstra, the programmer, would write software for the nonexistent machine. Two of the lessons he learned from this experience were the importance of clear documentation, and that program debugging can be largely avoided through careful design.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
Dijkstra formulated and solved the <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest path problem</a> for a demonstration at the official inauguration of the ARMAC computer in 1956. Because of the absence of journals dedicated to automatic computing, he did not publish the result until 1959.
</p><p>At the Mathematical Center, Dijkstra and his colleague <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jaap_Zonneveld&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jaap Zonneveld (page does not exist)">Jaap Zonneveld</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaap_Zonneveld" class="extiw" title="nl:Jaap Zonneveld">nl</a>&#93;</span> developed the first <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> for the programming language <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a> by August 1960, more than a year before a compiler was produced by another group.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> It had a profound influence on his later thinking on programming as a scientific activity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eindhoven_University_of_Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Eindhoven University of Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TU_Eindhoven.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/220px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/330px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/440px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TU_Eindhoven.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a> in the south of the Netherlands, where Dijkstra was a professor of mathematics from 1962 to 1984.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1962, Dijkstra moved to <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a>, and later to <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a>, in the south of the Netherlands, where he became a professor in the Mathematics Department at the <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The university did not have a separate computer science department and the culture of the mathematics department did not particularly suit him. Dijkstra tried to build a group of computer scientists who could collaborate on solving problems. This was an unusual model of research for the Mathematics Department.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In the late 1960s he built the <a href="/wiki/THE_multiprogramming_system" title="THE multiprogramming system">THE operating system</a> (named for the university, then known as <a href="/wiki/Technische_Hogeschool_Eindhoven" class="mw-redirect" title="Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven">Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven</a>), which has influenced the designs of subsequent <a href="/wiki/Operating_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating systems">operating systems</a> through its use of software based paged virtual memory.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burroughs_Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Burroughs Corporation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dijkstra joined <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</a>, a company known then for producing computers based on an innovative hardware architecture, as its <a href="/wiki/Research_fellow" title="Research fellow">research fellow</a> in August 1973. His duties consisted of visiting some of the firm's research centers a few times a year and carrying on his own research, which he did in the smallest Burroughs research facility, namely, his study on the second floor of his house in Nuenen. In fact, Dijkstra was the only research fellow of Burroughs and worked for it from home, occasionally travelling to its branches in the United States. As a result, he reduced his appointment at the university to one day a week. That day, Tuesday, soon became known as the day of the famous 'Tuesday Afternoon Club', a seminar during which he discussed with his colleagues scientific articles, looking at all aspects: notation, organisation, presentation, language, content, etc. Shortly after he moved in 1984 to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> (USA), a new 'branch' of the Tuesday Afternoon Club emerged in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Burroughs years saw him at his most prolific in output of research articles. He wrote nearly 500 documents in the EWD series (described below), most of them technical reports, for private circulation within a select group.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin">The University of Texas at Austin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The University of Texas at Austin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/220px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/330px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/440px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a>, where Dijkstra held the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in Computer Sciences from 1984 until 1999.</div></div></div>
<p>Dijkstra accepted the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in the Computer Science Department at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> in 1984.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dijkstra worked in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a> until his retirement  in November 1999. To mark the occasion and to celebrate his forty-plus years of seminal contributions to <a href="/wiki/Computing_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Computing science">computing science</a>, the Department of Computer Sciences organized a symposium, which took place on his 70th birthday in May 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dijkstra and his wife returned from Austin to his original house in <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a> (Netherlands) where he found that he had only months to live. He said that he wanted to retire in Austin, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, but to die in the Netherlands. Dijkstra died on 6 August 2002 after a long struggle with cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He and his wife Maria (Ria) Debets were survived by their three children: Marcus, Femke, and the computer scientist Rutger M. Dijkstra.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pioneering_contributions_and_impact_on_computing_science">Pioneering contributions and impact on computing science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pioneering contributions and impact on computing science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1006693578">.mw-parser-output .sidebar{width:22em;float:right;clear:right;margin:0.5em 0 1em 1em;background:#f8f9fa;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;display:table}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .sidebar{display:table!important;float:right!important;margin:0.5em 0 1em 1em!important}.mw-parser-output .sidebar a{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sidebar-wraplinks a{white-space:normal}.mw-parser-output .sidebar-subgroup{width:100%;margin:0;border-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .sidebar-left{float:left;clear:left;margin:0.5em 1em 1em 0}.mw-parser-output .sidebar-none{float:none;clear:both;margin:0.5em 1em 1em 0}.mw-parser-output .sidebar-outer-title{padding-bottom:0.2em;font-size:125%;line-height:1.2em;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output </tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r992953826">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox .navbar{display:block;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox-title .navbar{float:left;text-align:left;margin-right:0.5em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Programming_paradigms" title="Template:Programming paradigms"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Programming_paradigms" title="Template talk:Programming paradigms"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Programming_paradigms&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>From a theoretical physicist by training, Dijkstra became one of the most influential figures of computing science's founding generation.<sup id="cite_ref-Wirth2005_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wirth2005-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wirth2008-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LLamport2002-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_(2015)-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dolev2000_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolev2000-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stepanov_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stepanov-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaplante1996_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaplante1996-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaplante2008_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaplante2008-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> As an <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">early pioneer in many research areas of computing science</a>, he helped <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">shape the new discipline</a> from both an engineering and an <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">academic perspective</a>. Many of his papers are the source of new research areas. Many concepts that are now standard in computer science were first identified by Dijkstra and/or bear names coined by him. Several important problems were also first formulated and solved by him. A 1994 survey of over a thousand professors of computer science was conducted to obtain a list of 38 most influential scholarly papers in the field, and Dijkstra is the author of five papers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaplante1996_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaplante1996-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaplante2008_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaplante2008-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> At the age of 42, he became the first non-American, non-British, and <a href="/wiki/Continental_European" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental European">continental European</a> winner of the <a href="/wiki/Turing_Award" title="Turing Award">Turing Award</a>.
</p><p>During his forty-plus years as a computing scientist, which included positions in both academia and industry, Dijkstra made numerous seminal contributions to many areas of <a href="/wiki/Computing_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Computing science">computing science</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Compiler_construction" class="mw-redirect" title="Compiler construction">compiler construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a> (<a href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent programming">concurrent programming</a>), <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Programming_paradigm" title="Programming paradigm">programming paradigm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming methodology">methodology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Programming_language_research" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming language research">programming language research</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">program design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">program development</a>, <a href="/wiki/Program_verification" class="mw-redirect" title="Program verification">program verification</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_software_engineering" title="History of software engineering">software engineering principles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algorithm_design" class="mw-redirect" title="Algorithm design">algorithm design</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_computer_science" title="Philosophy of computer science">philosophical foundations of computer programming and computer science</a>. In addition, Dijkstra was <a href="/wiki/On_the_Cruelty_of_Really_Teaching_Computer_Science" title="On the Cruelty of Really Teaching Computer Science">intensely interested in teaching computer science</a>, and in the relationships between <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">academic computing science</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Software_industry" title="Software industry">software industry</a>.
</p><p>His main pioneering contributions (including ideas, inventions and innovations) include:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Algorithms</b>: <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%27s_algorithm" title="Dijkstra&#39;s algorithm">Dijkstra's algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/DJP_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="DJP algorithm">DJP algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra-Scholten_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Dijkstra-Scholten algorithm">Dijkstra-Scholten algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dekker%27s_algorithm" title="Dekker&#39;s algorithm">Dekker's algorithm</a> (generalization), <a href="/wiki/Banker%27s_algorithm" title="Banker&#39;s algorithm">banker's algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smoothsort" title="Smoothsort">smoothsort</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shunting-yard_algorithm" title="Shunting-yard algorithm">shunting-yard algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tracing_garbage_collection#tri-color_marking" title="Tracing garbage collection">tri-color marking algorithm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithm" title="Distributed algorithm">distributed algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deadlock_prevention_algorithms" title="Deadlock prevention algorithms">deadlock prevention algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilizing algorithms</a></li>
<li><b>Concepts, methods, principles and theories</b>: <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a> <a href="/wiki/Programming_language_implementation" title="Programming language implementation">implementation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Call_stack" title="Call stack">call stack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">concurrency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent programming">concurrent programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cooperating_sequential_processes" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooperating sequential processes">cooperating sequential processes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Critical_section" title="Critical section">critical section</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deadly_embrace" class="mw-redirect" title="Deadly embrace">deadly embrace</a> (<a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a>), <a href="/wiki/Dining_philosophers_problem" title="Dining philosophers problem">dining philosophers problem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_national_flag_problem" title="Dutch national flag problem">Dutch national flag problem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fault-tolerant_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Fault-tolerant system">fault-tolerant systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goto-less_programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Goto-less programming">goto-less programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guarded_Command_Language" title="Guarded Command Language">guarded command language</a> (<a href="/wiki/Guard_(computer_science)" title="Guard (computer science)">guarded commands</a>), <a href="/wiki/Abstraction_layer" title="Abstraction layer">layered structure</a> in <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">software architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abstraction_layer" title="Abstraction layer">levels of abstraction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multithreaded_programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Multithreaded programming">multithreaded programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> (<i>mutex</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Lock_(computer_science)" title="Lock (computer science)">lock</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Producer%E2%80%93consumer_problem" title="Producer–consumer problem">producer–consumer problem</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bounded_buffer_problem" class="mw-redirect" title="Bounded buffer problem">bounded buffer problem</a>), <a href="/wiki/Software_product_line" title="Software product line">program families</a>, <a href="/wiki/Predicate_transformer_semantics" science)">process synchronization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilizing_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-stabilizing system">self-stabilizing distributed systems</a> (<a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a>), <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">separation of concerns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sleeping_barber_problem" title="Sleeping barber problem">sleeping barber problem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis">structured analysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/THE_multiprogramming_system" title="THE multiprogramming system">THE multiprogramming system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unbounded_nondeterminism" title="Unbounded nondeterminism">unbounded nondeterminism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weakest_precondition_calculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Weakest precondition calculus">weakest precondition calculus</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algorithmic_work">Algorithmic work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Algorithmic work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">Shortest path problem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graph_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Graph algorithms">Graph algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent algorithm">Concurrent algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">Mutual exclusion algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deadlock_prevention_algorithms" title="Deadlock prevention algorithms">Deadlock prevention algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithm" title="Distributed algorithm">Distributed algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilizing_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-stabilizing algorithm">Self-stabilizing algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorting_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Sorting algorithms">Sorting algorithms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Parsing_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Parsing algorithms">Parsing algorithms</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra_Animation.gif" class="image"><img alt="Dijkstra&#39;s algorithm runtime" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Dijkstra_Animation.gif/220px-Dijkstra_Animation.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Dijkstra_Animation.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="283" data-file-height="222" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra_Animation.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dijkstra's algorithm. It picks the unvisited vertex with the lowest-distance, calculates the distance through it to each unvisited neighbor, and updates the neighbor's distance if smaller. Mark visited (set to red) when done with neighbors.</div></div></div>
<p>Dijkstra's algorithmic work (especially <a href="/wiki/Graph_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Graph algorithms">graph algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a>) plays an important role in many areas of computing science. According to <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Leslie Lamport</a> (2002), Dijkstra "started the field of concurrent and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a> with his 1965 CACM paper "Solution of a Problem in Concurrent Programming Control", in which he first stated and solved the mutual exclusion problem." As Lamport explains, "that paper is probably why <a href="/wiki/PODC" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC">PODC</a> exists (...). It remains to this day the most influential paper in the field. That it did not win a <a href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential Paper Award</a> reflects an artificial separation between concurrent and distributed algorithms–a separation that has never existed in Dijkstra's work."<sup id="cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LLamport2002-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1959 Dijkstra published in a 3-page article 'A note on two problems in connexion with graphs' the <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a> to find the shortest path in a graph between any two given nodes, now called <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%27s_algorithm" title="Dijkstra&#39;s algorithm">Dijkstra's algorithm</a>. Its impact over the next 40 years is summarised from the article of <a href="/wiki/Mikkel_Thorup" title="Mikkel Thorup">Mikkel Thorup</a>, 'Undirected Single Source Shortest Paths with Positive Integer Weights in Linear Time' (1999): "Since 1959, all theoretical developments in SSSP [Single-Source Shortest Paths] for general directed and undirected graphs have been based on Dijkstra's algorithm." Dijkstra's algorithm is used in SPF, <a href="/wiki/Shortest_Path_First" class="mw-redirect" title="Shortest Path First">Shortest Path First</a>, which is used in the routing protocols <a href="/wiki/Open_Shortest_Path_First" title="Open Shortest Path First">OSPF</a> and <a href="/wiki/IS-IS" title="IS-IS">IS-IS</a>. Various modifications to Dijkstra's algorithm have been proposed by many authors using <a href="/wiki/Heuristic_(computer_science)" title="Heuristic (computer science)">heuristics</a> to reduce the run time of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Shortest_path_search&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Shortest path search (page does not exist)">shortest path search</a>. One of the most used heuristic algorithms is the <a href="/wiki/A*_search_algorithm" title="A* search algorithm">A* search algorithm</a> (first described by <a href="/wiki/Peter_E._Hart" title="Peter E. Hart">Peter Hart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nils_John_Nilsson" title="Nils John Nilsson">Nils Nilsson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bertram_Raphael" title="Bertram Raphael">Bertram Raphael</a> of <a href="/wiki/SRI_International" title="SRI International">Stanford Research Institute</a> in 1968),<sup id="cite_ref-nilsson_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nilsson-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> the main goal is to reduce the run time by reducing the search space. Dijkstra thought about the <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest path problem</a> when working at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a> in 1956 as a <a href="/wiki/Computer_programmer" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer programmer">programmer</a> to demonstrate capabilities of a new <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a> named ARMAC. His objective was to choose both a problem and an answer (that would be produced by computer) that non-computing people could understand. He designed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%27s_algorithm" title="Dijkstra&#39;s algorithm">shortest path algorithm</a> in about 20 minutes without aid of paper and pen and later implemented it for ARMAC for a slightly simplified transportation map of 64 cities in the Netherlands (so that 6 bits would suffice to represent the city in the algorithm).<sup id="cite_ref-MacTutor.bio_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor.bio-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> As he recalled, in an interview published in 2001:<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>What is the shortest way to travel from Rotterdam to <a href="/wiki/Groningen" title="Groningen">Groningen</a>, in general: from given city to given city? <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">It is the algorithm for the shortest path</a>, which I designed in about twenty minutes. One morning I was shopping in Amsterdam with my young fiancée, and tired, we sat down on the café terrace to drink a cup of coffee and I was just thinking about whether I could do this, and I then designed the algorithm for the shortest path. As I said, it was a twenty-minute invention. In fact, it was published in ’59, three years late. The publication is still readable, it is, in fact, quite nice. One of the reasons that it is so nice was that I designed it without pencil and paper. I learned later that one of the advantages of designing without pencil and paper is that you are almost forced to avoid all avoidable complexities. Eventually that algorithm became, to my great amazement, one of the cornerstones of my fame.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Edsger Dijkstra, in an interview with Philip L. Frana, Communications of the ACM 53 (8), 2001.</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>A year later, he came across another problem from hardware engineers working on the institute's next computer: minimize the amount of wire needed to connect the pins on the back panel of the machine. As a solution, he rediscovered the algorithm known as <a href="/wiki/Prim%27s_algorithm" title="Prim&#39;s algorithm">Prim's</a> <a href="/wiki/Minimum_spanning_tree" title="Minimum spanning tree">minimal spanning tree algorithm</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Prim%27s_algorithm" title="Prim&#39;s algorithm">Prim's algorithm</a> was originally developed in 1930 by <a href="/wiki/Czech_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Czech people">Czech</a> mathematician <a href="/wiki/Vojt%C4%9Bch_Jarn%C3%ADk" title="Vojtěch Jarník">Vojtěch Jarník</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and later independently rediscovered and republished by <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Prim" title="Robert C. Prim">Robert C. Prim</a> in 1957,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and Dijkstra in 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, it is also sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/DJP_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="DJP algorithm">DJP algorithm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pr02_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pr02-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1961, Dijkstra first described the <a href="/wiki/Shunting-yard_algorithm" title="Shunting-yard algorithm">shunting-yard algorithm</a>, a method for parsing mathematical expressions specified in <a href="/wiki/Infix_notation" title="Infix notation">infix notation</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> report.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> It can be used to produce output in <a href="/wiki/Reverse_Polish_notation" title="Reverse Polish notation">Reverse Polish notation</a> (RPN) or as an <a href="/wiki/Abstract_syntax_tree" title="Abstract syntax tree">abstract syntax tree</a> (AST). The <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a> was named the <i>shunting yard</i> algorithm because its operation resembles that of a <a href="/wiki/Classification_yard" title="Classification yard">railroad shunting yard</a>. The shunting-yard algorithm is commonly used to implement <a href="/wiki/Operator-precedence_parser" title="Operator-precedence parser">operator-precedence parsers</a>.
</p><p>In 1962 or 1963, Dijkstra proposed the <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> mechanism for <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> algorithm for n processes (a generalization of <a href="/wiki/Dekker%27s_algorithm" title="Dekker&#39;s algorithm">Dekker's algorithm</a>), which was probably the first published <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithm</a> and which introduced a new area of algorithmic research. He also identified the <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> problem and proposed the <a href="/wiki/Banker%27s_algorithm" title="Banker&#39;s algorithm">banker's algorithm</a> that <a href="/wiki/Deadlock_prevention_algorithms" title="Deadlock prevention algorithms">prevents deadlock</a>.
</p><p>In 1974, Dijkstra presented three <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a> algorithms for mutual exclusion on a ring. This work is considered the first to introduce and demonstrate the self-stabilization concept.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDolev200016_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDolev200016-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-1970s, Dijkstra (together with other authors) introduced two useful abstractions (mutator and collector) to the study of <a href="/wiki/Garbage_collection_(computer_science)" title="Garbage collection (computer science)">garbage collection</a>. The mutator abstracts the process that performs the computation, including allocation of a new storage cell. The collector is the process that automatically reclaims garbage. Further, this paper gives a formalization of <i><a href="/wiki/Tracing_garbage_collection#Tri-color_marking" title="Tracing garbage collection">tri-color marking</a></i> that is basic to incremental garbage collection.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1980s, Dijkstra and <a href="/wiki/Carel_S._Scholten" title="Carel S. Scholten">Carel S. Scholten</a> proposed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%E2%80%93Scholten_algorithm" title="Dijkstra–Scholten algorithm">Dijkstra–Scholten algorithm</a> for detecting termination in <a href="/wiki/Distributed_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed systems">distributed systems</a>.
</p><p>In 1981, Dijkstra developed <a href="/wiki/Smoothsort" title="Smoothsort">smoothsort</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Comparison_sort" title="Comparison sort">comparison-based</a> <a href="/wiki/Sorting_algorithm" title="Sorting algorithm">sorting algorithm</a> and a variation of <a href="/wiki/Heapsort" title="Heapsort">heapsort</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compiler_construction_and_programming_language_research">Compiler construction and programming language research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Compiler construction and programming language research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrologica_X1" title="Electrologica X1">Electrologica X1</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Compiler_construction" class="mw-redirect" title="Compiler construction">Compiler construction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/220px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/330px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/440px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>By August 1960, Dijkstra and his colleague Zonneveld had completed the <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">implementation of the first ALGOL 60 compiler</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Electrologica_X1" title="Electrologica X1">Electrologica X1</a> computer.</div></div></div>
<p>Dijkstra was known to be a fan of the <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming language</a> <a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL</a>, and worked on the team that implemented the first <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> for <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a>, which he was closely involved in developing, realising, and popularising. As discussed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Naur" title="Peter Naur">Peter Naur</a> in the article 'The European side of the last phase of the development of ALGOL 60', in the <i>Proceedings of the First ACM SIGPLAN Conference on History of Programming Languages</i>, January 1978, Dijkstra took part in the period 1958–1959 in a number of meetings that culminated in the publication of the report defining the ALGOL 60 language. Dijkstra's name does not appear in the list of 13 authors of the final report, but he was an official member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_for_Information_Processing" title="International Federation for Information Processing">International Federation for Information Processing</a> (IFIP) <a href="/wiki/IFIP_Working_Group_2.1" title="IFIP Working Group 2.1">IFIP Working Group 2.1</a> on Algorithmic Languages and Calculi,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> which specified, supports, and maintains ALGOL 60 and <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_68" title="ALGOL 68">ALGOL 68</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> He eventually left the committee, apparently because he could not agree with the majority opinions. Still, while at the Mathematisch Centrum (Amsterdam), he wrote jointly with Jaap Zonneveld the <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">first ALGOL 60 compiler</a>. Dijkstra and Zonneveld, who collaborated on the compiler, agreed not to shave until the project was completed. Zonneveld shaved shortly thereafter, but Dijkstra kept his beard for the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL</a> was the result of a collaboration of American and European committees. <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a> (short for ALGOrithmic Language 1960) is a member of the ALGOL family of computer programming languages. It followed on from <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_58" title="ALGOL 58">ALGOL 58</a> and inspired many languages that followed it. It gave rise to many other languages, including <a href="/wiki/BCPL" title="BCPL">BCPL</a>, <a href="/wiki/B_(programming_language)" title="B (programming language)">B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simula" title="Simula">Simula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Algol 60 was a sophisticatedly designed <a href="/wiki/Computer_language" title="Computer language">computer language</a> and it provided a large number of hitherto unknown <a href="/wiki/Programming_language_implementation" title="Programming language implementation">implementation</a> challenges. As <a href="/wiki/Bjarne_Stroustrup" title="Bjarne Stroustrup">Bjarne Stroustrup</a> notes, "one problem with Algol60 was that no one knew how to implement it."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> A major new challenge in <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">Algol 60 implementation</a> was the run-time allocation and management of data. In 1960, Dijkstra and Zonneveld showed how recursive procedures could be executed using a run-time <a href="/wiki/Stack_(abstract_data_type)" title="Stack (abstract data type)">stack</a> of activation records, and how to efficiently access identifiers from statically enclosing scopes using the so-called <i>display</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The ALGOL 60 compiler was one of the first to support <a href="/wiki/Recursion_(computer_science)" title="Recursion (computer science)">recursion</a><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> employing a novel method to do so. Dijkstra's short book <i>Primer of Algol 60 Programming</i>, originally published in 1962, was the standard reference for the language for several years.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_programming_methodology_and_the_birth_of_the_structured_programming_movement">Role of programming methodology and the birth of the structured programming movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Role of programming methodology and the birth of the structured programming movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Software_crisis" title="Software crisis">Software crisis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming methodology">Programming methodology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goto-less_programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Goto-less programming">Goto-less programming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">Structured programming</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In 1965 Dijkstra wrote his famous <i>Notes on Structured Programming</i> and declared <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">programming</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" title="Academic discipline">discipline</a> in contrast to a <a href="/wiki/Craft" title="Craft">craft</a>. Also in 1965 <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Hoare</a> published an important paper about data structuring. These ideas had a profound influence on new <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming language</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>. Languages are the vehicles in which these ideas were to be expressed. <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">Structured programming</a> became supported by a <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured programming language</a>.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Niklaus_Wirth" title="Niklaus Wirth">Niklaus Wirth</a>, <i>IEEE Annals of the History of Computing</i> (2008)<sup id="cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wirth2008-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The revolution in views of programming started by Dijkstra's iconoclasm led to a movement known as structured programming, which advocated a systematic, rational approach to program construction. Structured programming is the basis for all that has been done since in <a href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a>.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Meyer" title="Bertrand Meyer">Bertrand Meyer</a>, <i>Touch of Class: Learning to Program Well with Objects and Contracts</i> (2009)<sup id="cite_ref-Meyer2009p188_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2009p188-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">Computer programming</a> in the 1950s to 1960s was not recognized as an <a href="/wiki/Discipline_(academia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Discipline (academia)">academic discipline</a> and unlike mature sciences there were no theoretical concepts or coding systems. Programming as a professional activity was poorly understood in those years. In the late 1960s computer programming was in state of crisis. <a href="/wiki/Software_crisis" title="Software crisis">Software crisis</a> is a term used in the early days of <a href="/wiki/Computing_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Computing science">computing science</a> for the difficulty of writing useful and efficient <a href="/wiki/Computer_programs" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer programs">computer programs</a> in the required time. The software crisis was due to the rapid increases in computer power and the complexity of the problems that could be tackled. With the increase in the complexity of the <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a>, many software problems arose because existing methods were insufficient. The term "software crisis" was coined by some attendees at the first of the <a href="/wiki/NATO_Software_Engineering_Conferences" title="NATO Software Engineering Conferences">NATO Software Engineering Conferences</a> in 1968 at <a href="/wiki/Garmisch" class="mw-redirect" title="Garmisch">Garmisch</a>, Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nato_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nato-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nato2_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nato2-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> His 1972 ACM Turing Award Lecture makes reference to this same problem: "The major cause of the software crisis is that the machines have become several orders of magnitude more powerful! To put it quite bluntly: as long as there were no machines, programming was no problem at all; when we had a few weak computers, programming became a mild problem, and now we have gigantic computers, programming has become an equally gigantic problem."<sup id="cite_ref-ewd340_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Dijkstra had programmed extensively in machine code in the 1950s, he came to the conclusion that in high-level languages frequent use of the <a href="/wiki/GOTO" class="mw-redirect" title="GOTO">GOTO</a> statement was usually symptomatic of poor structure. In 1968 he wrote a private paper "A Case against the GO TO Statement",<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> which was then published as a letter in CACM.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Editor <a href="/wiki/Niklaus_Wirth" title="Niklaus Wirth">Niklaus Wirth</a> gave this letter the heading "<a href="/wiki/Go_To_Statement_Considered_Harmful" class="mw-redirect" title="Go To Statement Considered Harmful">Go To Statement Considered Harmful</a>", which introduced the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Considered_harmful" title="Considered harmful">considered harmful</a>" into computing.
</p><p>Dijkstra argued that the programming statement GOTO, found in many high-level programming languages, is a major source of errors, and should therefore be eliminated. This letter caused a huge debate in the programming community. Some went to the length of equating good programming with the elimination of <a href="/wiki/GO_TO" class="mw-redirect" title="GO TO">GO TO</a>. Dijkstra refused to mention the debate, or even the GO TO statement, in his article "Notes on Structured Programming". The debate has long since died down; programming languages provide alternatives to the GO TO, few programmers today use it liberally, and most never use it at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dijkstra's thesis was that departures from linear control flow were clearer if allowed only in disciplined higher-level structures such as the <a href="/wiki/Conditional_(computer_programming)" title="Conditional (computer programming)">if-then-else</a> statement and the <a href="/wiki/While_loop" title="While loop">while loop</a>. This methodology was developed into <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming movement</a>, the title of his 1972 book, coauthored with <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Tony Hoare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ole-Johan_Dahl" title="Ole-Johan Dahl">Ole-Johan Dahl</a>. Considered by many as the first significant movement in the history of computer programming, structured programming became the new programming orthodoxy during the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnuth1974_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnuth1974-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">Structured programming</a> is often regarded as "goto-less programming". But as Bertrand Meyer notes, "As the first book on the topic [<i>Structured Programming</i> by Dijkstra, Dahl, and Hoare] shows, structured programming is about much more than control structures and the <a href="/wiki/Goto" title="Goto">goto</a>. Its principal message is that programming should be considered a scientific discipline based on mathematical rigor."<sup id="cite_ref-Meyer2009p188_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2009p188-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">As a programming paradigm</a>, structured programming – especially in the 1970s and 1980s – significantly influenced the birth of many modern <a href="/wiki/Programming_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming languages">programming languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Wirth2005_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wirth2005-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wirth2008-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modula-2" title="Modula-2">Modula-2</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ada_(programming_language)" title="Ada (programming language)">Ada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fortran_77" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortran 77">Fortran 77</a> version which incorporates the concepts of structured programming, was released in 1978. The <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> language was a considerably extended and enhanced version of the popular <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured programming language</a> <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a> (see also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_C-based_programming_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="List of C-based programming languages">list of C-based programming languages</a>). Since C++ was developed from a more traditional <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured language</a>, it is a '<a href="/wiki/Multi-paradigm_programming_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-paradigm programming language">hybrid language</a>', rather than a pure <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a> language.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his article <i>Structured Programming: Retrospect and Prospect</i> (1986), <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Mills" title="Harlan Mills">Harlan Mills</a> writes, "Edsger W. Dijkstra's 1969 <i>Structured Programming</i> article precipitated a decade of intense focus on programming techniques that has fundamentally altered human expectations and achievements in <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">software development</a>. Before this decade of intense focus, programming was regarded as a private, puzzle-solving activity of writing computer instructions to work as a program. After this decade, programming could be regarded as a public, mathematics-based activity of restructuring specifications into programs. Before, the challenge was in getting programs to run at all, and then in getting them further debugged to do the right things. After, programs could be expected to both run and do the right things with little or no debugging. Before, it was common wisdom that no sizable program could be error-free. After, many sizable programs have run a year or more with no errors detected. These expectations and achievements are not universal because of the inertia of industrial practices. But they are well-enough established to herald fundamental change in software development."
</p><p>The book <i>Concise Encyclopedia of Computer Science</i> (2004), edited by Edwin D. Reilly, notes that "the major contributions of structured programming have been twofold—the elevation of programming technique to something less of an art and more of a science, and the demonstration that carefully structured programs can be creative works of sufficient literary merit to deserve being read by humans and not just by computer."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReilly2004734_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReilly2004734-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_on_program_design_and_development_at_the_dawn_of_software_engineering">Research on program design and development at the dawn of software engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Research on program design and development at the dawn of software engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_software_engineering" title="History of software engineering">History of software engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">Software architecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">Separation of concerns</a></div>
<p>Dijkstra's ideas about <a href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a> (especially the structured programming movement) helped lay the foundations for the birth and development of the professional discipline of <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> (in particular the software design and development), enabling programmers to organize and manage increasingly complex software projects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroyDenert2002&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2020&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2020)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_64-0" class="reference"><a
