href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroyDenert2002[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2020]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2020)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenderson2009&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2020&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2020)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenderson2009[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2020]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2020)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In the late 1960s Dijkstra discussed the concept of program families. In the mid 1970s, <a href="/wiki/David_Parnas" title="David Parnas">David Parnas</a> and others clarified the idea and showed how to apply it in software engineering principles.
</p><p>The rise of the <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a> movement led to many other <i><a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis">structured</a></i> approaches applied to <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">software design</a>. The techniques of <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis">structured analysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Structured_design" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured design">structured design</a> are outgrowths of structured programming concepts and techniques, and of the early ideas about modular design. Principles of modularity were strengthened by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Constantine" title="Larry Constantine">Larry Constantine</a>'s concepts of <a href="/wiki/Coupling_(computer_programming)" title="Coupling (computer programming)">coupling</a> (to be minimized between modules) and <a href="/wiki/Cohesion_(computer_science)" title="Cohesion (computer science)">cohesion</a> (to be maximized within modules), by <a href="/wiki/David_Parnas" title="David Parnas">David Parnas</a>'s techniques of <a href="/wiki/Information_hiding" title="Information hiding">information hiding</a>, and by <a href="/wiki/Abstract_data_types" class="mw-redirect" title="Abstract data types">abstract data types</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> A number of tools and methods employing <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis">structured concepts</a> were developed, such as <a href="/wiki/Structured_Design" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured Design">Structured Design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Structured_Programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Jackson Structured Programming">Jackson's Structured Programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_T._Ross" title="Douglas T. Ross">Ross</a>' <a href="/wiki/Structured_Analysis_and_Design_Technique" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured Analysis and Design Technique">Structured Analysis and Design Technique</a> (SADT), <a href="/wiki/Edward_Yourdon#Yourdon_Structured_Method" title="Edward Yourdon">Yourdon's Structured Method</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structured_Systems_Analysis_and_Design_Method" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured Systems Analysis and Design Method">Structured Systems Analysis and Design Method</a> (SSADM), and James Martin's <a href="/wiki/Information_technology_engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Information technology engineering">information technology engineering</a>. The field of <a href="/wiki/Software_metrics" class="mw-redirect" title="Software metrics">software metrics</a> is often considered as a direct influence of the structured programming movement on <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> in the 1970s.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">Separation of concerns</a> (SoC), one of the basic principles in <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a>, is a design principle for separating a <a href="/wiki/Computer_program" title="Computer program">computer program</a> into distinct sections, such that each section addresses a separate <a href="/wiki/Concern_(computer_science)" title="Concern (computer science)">concern</a>. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">separation of concerns</a></i> was coined by Dijkstra in his 1974 paper "On the role of scientific thought".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_system_research">Operating system research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Operating system research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/THE_multiprogramming_system" title="THE multiprogramming system">THE multiprogramming system</a></div>
<p>In the 1960s Dijkstra and his colleagues in <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a> designed and implemented <a href="/wiki/THE_multiprogramming_system" title="THE multiprogramming system">THE</a> (standing for '<a href="/wiki/Technische_Hogeschool_Eindhoven" class="mw-redirect" title="Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven">Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven</a>') <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a>, which was organised into clearly identified <a href="/wiki/Abstraction_layer" title="Abstraction layer">abstraction layers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> His 1968 article on this subject provided the foundation for subsequent designs of the operating systems. The IEEE Computer Society's <a href="/wiki/David_Alan_Grier_(writer)" title="David Alan Grier (writer)">David Alan Grier</a> writes, "We generally trace the idea of building computer systems in layers back to a 1967 paper that the Dutch computer scientist Edsger Dijkstra gave to a joint IEEE Computer Society/ACM conference. Prior to this paper, engineers had struggled with the problem of how to organize software. If you look at early examples of programs, and you can find many in the electronic library of the Computer Society, you will find that most code of that era is complicated, difficult to read, hard to modify, and challenging to reuse. In his 1967 paper, Dijkstra described how software could be constructed in layers and gave an example of a simple operating system that used five layers. He admitted that this system might not be a realistic test of his ideas but he argued that the "larger the project, the more essential the structuring!" The idea of using layers to control complexity has become a mainstay of software architecture. We see it in many forms and apply it to many problems. We see it in the hierarchy of classes in object-oriented programming and in the structure of <a href="/wiki/Service-oriented_architecture" title="Service-oriented architecture">service-oriented architecture</a> (SOA). SOA is a relatively recent application of layering in computer science. It was articulated in 2007 as a means of controlling complexity in business systems, especially distributed systems that make substantial use of the Internet. Like Dijkstra's plan for system development, its layering system is called the SOA Solution Stack or S3. The S3's nine layers are: 1) operational systems, 2) service components, 3) services, 4) business processes, 5) consumer actions, 6) system integration, 7) quality control and assurance, 8) information architecture, and 9) system governance and policies."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dijkstra organized the design of the system in layers in order to reduce the overall complexity of the software. Though the term 'architecture' had not yet been used to describe <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">software design</a>, this was certainly considered the first glimpse of <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">software architecture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Albin03_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Albin03-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> It introduced a number of design principles which have become part of the working vocabulary of every professional programmer: levels of abstraction, programming in layers, the semaphore, and <a href="/wiki/Cooperating_sequential_processes" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooperating sequential processes">cooperating sequential processes</a>. His original paper on the THE operating system was reprinted in the 25th Anniversary issue of Communications of the ACM, in January 1983. By way of introduction, the Editor-in-Chief says, "This project initiated a long line of research in multilevel systems architecture — a line that continues to the present day because hierarchical modularity is a powerful approach to organizing large systems."<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Concurrent_computing_.28concurrent_programming.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Concurrent_computing_(concurrent_programming)">Concurrent computing (concurrent programming)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Concurrent computing (concurrent programming)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">Concurrency (computer science)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">Concurrent computing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent programming">Concurrent programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent algorithm">Concurrent algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dining_philosophers_problem" title="Dining philosophers problem">Dining philosophers problem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">Mutual exclusion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">Semaphore (programming)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cooperating_sequential_processes" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooperating sequential processes">Cooperating sequential processes</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>While <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent programming">concurrent program</a> execution had been considered for years, the <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">computer science of concurrency</a> began with Edsger Dijkstra's seminal 1965 paper that introduced the <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem. (...) The second fundamental concurrent programming problem to be studied was producer-consumer synchronization. This form of <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_(computer_science)" title="Synchronization (computer science)">synchronization</a> was used at the hardware level in the earliest computers, but it was first identified as a concurrency problem by Dijkstra in 1965, though not published in this formulation until 1968. (...) After <a href="/wiki/Distributed_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed system">distributed systems</a>, the next major step in <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithms</a> was the study of <a href="/wiki/Fault_tolerance" title="Fault tolerance">fault tolerance</a>. The first scientific examination of fault tolerance was Dijkstra's seminal 1974 paper on <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a>. However, as sometimes happens with work that is ahead of its time, that paper received little attention and was essentially forgotten for a decade. (...) The ensuing decades have seen a huge growth of interest in concurrency particularly in distributed systems. Looking back at the origins of the field, what stands out is the fundamental role played by Edsger Dijkstra, to whom this history is dedicated.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Leslie Lamport</a>, <i>Turing Lecture: The Computer Science of Concurrency: The Early Years</i> (June 2015)<sup id="cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_(2015)-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/170px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/255px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/340px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="3072" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <i lang="nl">seinpaal</i>), the term used in Dijkstra's original paper.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In the early 1960s Dijkstra proposed the first synchronisation mechanism for concurrent processes, the <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> with its two operations, P and V.</div></div></div>
<p>In a one-page paper from 1965 Dijkstra introduced the '<a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem' for n processes and discussed a solution to it. It was probably the first published <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_(2015)-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The notion, standard by now, of a '<a href="/wiki/Critical_section" title="Critical section">critical section</a>' was also introduced in this paper. <a href="/wiki/Per_Brinch_Hansen" title="Per Brinch Hansen">Per Brinch Hansen</a>, a pioneer in the field of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a>, considers Dijkstra's <i>Cooperating Sequential Processes</i> (1965) to be the <a href="/wiki/List_of_important_publications_in_concurrent,_parallel,_and_distributed_computing" title="List of important publications in concurrent, parallel, and distributed computing">first classic paper</a> in concurrent programming. As Brinch Hansen notes, 'Dijkstra lays the conceptual foundation for abstract concurrent programming' with that paper.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrinch_Hansen20028_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrinch_Hansen20028-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/220px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/330px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/440px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png 2x" data-file-width="2550" data-file-height="1808" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simple example of two processes modifying a linked list at the same time causing a conflict. The requirement of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> was first identified and solved by Dijkstra in his seminal 1965 paper titled <i>Solution of a problem in concurrent programming control</i>, and is credited as the first topic in the study of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithms</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1968 Dijkstra published his seminal paper '<a href="/wiki/Cooperating_sequential_processes" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooperating sequential processes">Cooperating sequential processes</a>', a 70-page essay that originated the field of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent programming">concurrent programming</a>. He discussed in it the notion of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mutex" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutex">mutex</a>) and the criteria a satisfactory solution should satisfy. He also redressed the historical perspective left out of his 1965 paper by including the <a href="/wiki/Dekker%27s_algorithm" title="Dekker&#39;s algorithm">first known correct solution to the mutual exclusion problem</a>, for two processes, due to <a href="/wiki/Theodorus_Dekker" title="Theodorus Dekker">Theodorus Dekker</a>. Dijkstra subsequently generalized <a href="/wiki/Dekker%27s_algorithm" title="Dekker&#39;s algorithm">Dekker's solution</a> to n processes.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Further, he proposed the first <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_(computer_science)" title="Synchronization (computer science)">synchronisation mechanism</a> for concurrent processes,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> with its two operations, P and V. He also identified the '<a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> problem' (called there 'the problem of the <a href="/wiki/Deadly_embrace" class="mw-redirect" title="Deadly embrace">deadly embrace</a>')<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and proposed an elegant '<a href="/wiki/Banker%27s_algorithm" title="Banker&#39;s algorithm">Banker's algorithm</a>' that <a href="/wiki/Deadlock_prevention_algorithms" title="Deadlock prevention algorithms">prevents deadlock</a>. The deadlock detection and prevention became perennial research problems in the field of concurrent programming.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png/220px-An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png/330px-An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png/440px-An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png 2x" data-file-width="1130" data-file-height="1172" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:An_illustration_of_the_dining_philosophers_problem.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the dining philosophers problem</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dining_philosophers_problem" title="Dining philosophers problem">dining philosophers problem</a> is an example problem often used in <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">concurrent</a> algorithm design to illustrate <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_(computer_science)" title="Synchronization (computer science)">synchronization</a> issues and techniques for resolving them. It was originally formulated in 1965 by Dijkstra as a student exam exercise, presented in terms of computers <a href="/wiki/Resource_contention" title="Resource contention">competing for access</a> to <a href="/wiki/Tape_drive" title="Tape drive">tape drive</a> peripherals. Soon after, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Tony Hoare</a> gave the problem its present formulation.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sleeping_barber_problem" title="Sleeping barber problem">sleeping barber problem</a> is also attributed to Dijkstra.
</p><p>Michel Raynal (2012) writes, "Since the early work of E.W. Dijkstra (1965), who introduced the mutual exclusion problem, the concept of a process, the semaphore object, the notion of a <a href="/wiki/Weakest_precondition" class="mw-redirect" title="Weakest precondition">weakest precondition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guarded_commands" class="mw-redirect" title="Guarded commands">guarded commands</a> (among many other contributions), <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_(computer_science)" title="Synchronization (computer science)">synchronization</a> is no longer a catalog of tricks but a domain of computing science with its own concepts, mechanisms, and techniques whose results can be applied in many domains. This means that process synchronization has to be a major topic of any computer science curriculum."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>John W. McCormick et al. (2011) notes, "The notion of the <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Concurrent program">concurrent program</a> as a means for writing <a href="/wiki/Parallel_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Parallel program">parallel programs</a> without regard for the underlying hardware was first introduced by Edsger Dijkstra (1968). <a href="/wiki/Moti_Ben-Ari" class="mw-redirect" title="Moti Ben-Ari">Moti Ben-Ari</a> (1982) elegantly summed up Dijkstra's idea in three sentences: ‘Concurrent programming is the name given to programming notation and techniques for expressing potential parallelism and solving the resulting synchronization and communication problems. Implementation of parallelism is a topic in computer systems (hardware and software) that is essentially independent of concurrent programming. Concurrent programming is important because it provides an abstract setting in which to study parallelism without getting bogged down in the implementation details.’"<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Distributed_computing_.28distributed_programming.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Distributed_computing_(distributed_programming)">Distributed computing (distributed programming)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Distributed computing (distributed programming)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">Distributed computing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distributed_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed systems">Distributed systems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">Self-stabilization</a></div>
<p>Dijkstra was one of the very early pioneers of the research on principles of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DijkstraPrize_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraPrize-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra_Prize" title="Dijkstra Prize">Dijkstra Prize</a> sponsors recognize, "no other individual has had a larger influence on research in principles of distributed computing."<sup id="cite_ref-DijkstraPrize_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraPrize-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Some of his papers are even considered to be those that established the field. Dijkstra's 1965 paper, <i>Solution of a Problem in Concurrent Programming Control</i> was the first to present the correct solution to the <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem. <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Leslie Lamport</a> writes that this work "is probably why <a href="/wiki/PODC" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC">PODC</a> exists" and it "started the field of concurrent and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LLamport2002-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In particular, his paper "Self-stabilizing Systems in Spite of Distributed Control" (1974) started the sub-field of <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a>. It is also considered as the first scientific examination of <a href="/wiki/Fault-tolerant_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Fault-tolerant systems">fault-tolerant systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_(2015)-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Dijkstra's paper was not widely noticed until Leslie Lamport's invited talk at the ACM <a href="/wiki/Symposium_on_Principles_of_Distributed_Computing" title="Symposium on Principles of Distributed Computing">Symposium on Principles of Distributed Computing</a> (<a href="/wiki/PODC" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC">PODC</a>) in 1983. In his report on Dijkstra's work on <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilizing</a> <a href="/wiki/Distributed_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed systems">distributed systems</a>, Lamport regard it to be 'a milestone in work on <a href="/wiki/Fault_tolerance" title="Fault tolerance">fault tolerance</a>' and 'a very fertile field for research'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDolev20003_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDolev20003-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_specification_and_verification">Formal specification and verification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Formal specification and verification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the 1970s, Dijkstra's chief interest was <a href="/wiki/Formal_verification" title="Formal verification">formal verification</a>. In 1976 Dijkstra published a seminal book, <i>A Discipline of Programming</i>, which put forward his method of systematic development of programs together with their correctness proofs. In his exposition he used his '<a href="/wiki/Guarded_Command_Language" title="Guarded Command Language">Guarded Command Language</a>'. The language, with its reliance on non-determinism, the adopted <a href="/wiki/Weakest_precondition" class="mw-redirect" title="Weakest precondition">weakest precondition</a> <a href="/wiki/Predicate_transformer_semantics" title="Predicate transformer semantics">semantics</a> and the proposed development method has had a considerable impact on the field to this day. The <a href="/wiki/Refinement_calculus" title="Refinement calculus">refinement calculus</a>, originally proposed by <a href="/wiki/Ralph-Johan_Back" title="Ralph-Johan Back">Ralph-Johan Back</a><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> and developed by <a href="/wiki/Carroll_Morgan_(computer_scientist)" title="Carroll Morgan (computer scientist)">Carroll Morgan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> is an extension of Dijkstra's <a href="/wiki/Weakest_precondition_calculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Weakest precondition calculus">weakest precondition calculus</a>, where program statements are modeled as <a href="/wiki/Predicate_transformer_semantics" title="Predicate transformer semantics">predicate transformers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBackvon_Wright2012v_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBackvon_Wright2012v-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1984, to add further support to this approach to programming, he published jointly with Wim Feijen an introductory textbook for first-year students of computer science. The book, first published in Dutch, was entitled <i>Een methode van programmeren</i>. The English edition appeared in 1988 as <i>A Method of Programming</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_nature_of_computer_science_and_computer_programming">On the nature of computer science and computer programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: On the nature of computer science and computer programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_computer_science" title="Philosophy of computer science">Philosophy of computer science</a></div>
<p>Many of his opinions on computer science and programming have become widespread. For example, the programming phrase "two or more, use a for" (a rule of thumb when to use a loop) is sometimes attributed to him.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He was the first to make the claim that programming is so inherently complex that, in order to manage it successfully, programmers need to harness every trick and abstraction possible.
</p><p>Dijkstra was one of the most famous opponents of the engineering view of computing science. Like <a href="/wiki/Peter_Naur" title="Peter Naur">Peter Naur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kristen_Nygaard" title="Kristen Nygaard">Kristen Nygaard</a>, Dijkstra disliked the very term 'computer science'. <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">Computer science</a>, as Dijkstra pointed out, deserves a better name. He suggests it can be called 'computing science'. Instead of the computer, or computing technology, Dijkstra wanted to emphasize the abstract mechanisms that computing science uses to master complexity. When expressing the abstract nature of <a href="/wiki/Computing_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Computing science">computing science</a>, he wrote,
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A confusion of even longer standing came from the fact that the unprepared included the electronic engineers that were supposed to design, build and maintain the machines. The job was actually beyond the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic technology">electronic technology</a> of the day, and, as a result, the question of how to get and keep the physical equipment more or less in working condition became in the early days the all-overriding concern. As a result, the topic became – primarily in the USA – prematurely known as ‘<a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>’ – which, actually, is like referring to <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a> as ‘knife science’ – and it was firmly implanted in people's minds that <a href="/wiki/Computing_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Computing science">computing science</a> is about machines and their peripheral equipment. Quod non [Latin: "Which is not true"]. We now know that electronic technology has no more to contribute to <a href="/wiki/Computing" title="Computing">computing</a> than the physical equipment. We now know that <a href="/wiki/Programmable_computer" class="mw-redirect" title="Programmable computer">programmable computer</a> is no more and no less than an extremely handy device for realizing any conceivable mechanism without changing a single wire, and that the core challenge for computing science is hence a conceptual one, viz., what (abstract) mechanisms we can conceive without getting lost in the complexities of our own making.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In <i>The Humble Programmer</i> (1972), Dijkstra wrote: "We must not forget that it is not our [computing scientists'] business to make programs, it is our business to design classes of computations that will display a desired behaviour."
</p><p>Dijkstra also opposed the inclusion of <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> under the umbrella of academic computer science. He wrote that, "As economics is known as "The Miserable Science", software engineering should be known as "The Doomed Discipline", doomed because it cannot even approach its goal since its goal is self-contradictory." And "software engineering has accepted as its charter "How to program if you cannot."."<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality_and_working_style">Personality and working style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Personality and working style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg/220px-Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg/330px-Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg/440px-Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3574" data-file-height="2447" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edsger_Dijkstra_1994.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dijkstra at the blackboard during a conference at <a href="/wiki/ETH_Zurich" title="ETH Zurich">ETH Zurich</a> in 1994. He once remarked, "<a href="/wiki/A_picture_is_worth_a_thousand_words" title="A picture is worth a thousand words">A picture may be worth a thousand words</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Formula" title="Formula">formula</a> is worth a thousand pictures."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Simplicity is a great virtue but it requires hard work to achieve it and education to appreciate it. And to make matters worse: complexity sells better. The computing industry is not the only one that has discovered that sore truth: so has the academic world. If you deliver a lecture that is crystal clear from the beginning to end, your audience feels cheated and mutters while leaving the lecture hall "That was all rather trivial, wasn't it?"</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>E.W. Dijkstra, "On the Nature of Computing Science" (EWD896), August 1984</cite></div></blockquote>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>You can hardly blame <a href="/wiki/M.I.T." class="mw-redirect" title="M.I.T.">M.I.T.</a> for not taking notice of an obscure computer scientist in a small town in the Netherlands.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Dijkstra used to wittily describe himself, in <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a> in the mid-1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DijkstraNYT_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraNYT-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In the world of computing science, Dijkstra is well known as a "character". In the preface of his book <i>A Discipline of Programming</i> (1976) he stated the following: "For the absence of a bibliography I offer neither explanation nor apology." In fact, most of his articles and books have no references at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This approach to references was deplored by some researchers. But Dijkstra chose this way of working to preserve his self-reliance.
</p><p>As a university professor for much of his life, Dijkstra saw teaching not just as a required activity but as a serious research endeavor.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> His approach to teaching was unconventional.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> His lecturing style has been described as idiosyncratic. When lecturing, the long pauses between sentences have often been attributed to the fact that English is not Dijkstra's first language. However the pauses also served as a way for him to think on his feet and he was regarded as a quick and deep thinker while engaged in the act of lecturing. His courses for students in Austin had little to do with computer science but they dealt with the presentation of mathematical proofs.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> At the beginning of each <a href="/wiki/Semester" class="mw-redirect" title="Semester">semester</a> he would take a photo of each of the students, in order to memorize their names. He never followed a <a href="/wiki/Textbook" title="Textbook">textbook</a>, with the possible exception of his own while it was under preparation. When lecturing, he would write proofs in chalk on a blackboard rather than using overhead foils. He invited the students to suggest ideas, which he then explored, or refused to explore because they violated some of his tenets. He assigned challenging homework problems, and would study his students' solutions thoroughly. He conducted his final examinations orally, over a whole week. Each student was examined in Dijkstra's office or home, and an exam lasted several hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is not the task of the University to offer what society asks for, but to give what society needs. [The things society asks for are generally understood, and you don't need a University for that; the university has to offer what no one else can provide.]</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>E.W. Dijkstra, "Answers to questions from students of Software Engineering" (EWD1305), November 2000</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Dijkstra was also highly original in his way of assessing people's capacity for a job. When <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lifschitz" title="Vladimir Lifschitz">Vladimir Lifschitz</a> came to Austin in 1990 for a job interview, Dijkstra gave him a puzzle. Vladimir solved it and has been working in Austin since then.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite having invented much of the technology of software, he eschewed the use of computers in his own work for many decades. Even after he succumbed to his UT colleagues' encouragement and acquired a <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">Macintosh</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a>, he used it only for <a href="/wiki/E-mail" class="mw-redirect" title="E-mail">e-mail</a> and for browsing the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UTx_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UTx-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Dijkstra never wrote his articles using a computer. He preferred to rely on his <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">typewriter</a> and later on his <a href="/wiki/Montblanc_(company)" title="Montblanc (company)">Montblanc</a> pen.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Dijkstra's favorite writing instrument was the Montblanc <a href="/wiki/Meisterst%C3%BCck" title="Meisterstück">Meisterstück</a> <a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">fountain pen</a>. He repeatedly tried other pens, but none ever displaced the Montblanc.
</p><p>He had no use for <a href="/wiki/Word_processors" class="mw-redirect" title="Word processors">word processors</a>, believing that one should be able to write a letter or article without rough drafts, rewriting, or any significant editing. He would work it all out in his head before putting <a href="/wiki/Pen" title="Pen">pen</a> to paper, and once mentioned that when he was a physics student he would solve his homework problems in his head while walking the streets of <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
Most of Dijkstra's publications were written by him alone. He never had a secretary and took care of all his correspondence alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> When colleagues prepared a <a href="/wiki/Festschrift" title="Festschrift">Festschrift</a> for his sixtieth birthday, published by <a href="/wiki/Springer-Verlag" class="mw-redirect" title="Springer-Verlag">Springer-Verlag</a>, he took the trouble to thank each of the 61 contributors separately, in a hand-written letter.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout Dijkstra's career, his work was characterized by elegance and economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> A prolific writer (especially as an essayist), Dijkstra authored more than 1,300 papers, many written by hand in his precise script. They were essays and parables; fairy tales and warnings; comprehensive explanation and pedagogical pretext. Most were about mathematics and computer science; others were trip reports that are more revealing about their author than about the people and places visited. It was his habit to copy each paper and circulate it to a small group of colleagues who would copy and forward the papers to another limited group of scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIstrail2008_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIstrail2008-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>  His love affair with <a href="/wiki/Simplicity" title="Simplicity">simplicity</a> came at an early age and under his mother's guidance. He once said he had asked his mother whether trigonometry was a difficult topic. She replied that he must learn all the formulas and that further, if he required more than five lines to prove something, he was on the wrong track.<sup id="cite_ref-Denken_als_Discpline_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denken_als_Discpline-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dijkstra was famous for his wit, eloquence, rudeness, abruptness and often cruelty to fellow professionals, and way with words, such as in his remark, "The question of whether Machines Can Think (…) is about as relevant as the question of whether Submarines Can Swim.".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> His advice to a promising researcher, who asked how to select a topic for research, was the wise words: "Do only what only you can do".<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Dijkstra was also known for his vocal criticism and absence of social skills when interacting with colleagues. As an outspoken and critical visionary, he strongly opposed the teaching of <a href="/wiki/BASIC" title="BASIC">BASIC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EWD498_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWD498-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In many of his more witty essays, Dijkstra described a fictional company of which he served as chairman. The company was called Mathematics, Inc., a company that he imagined having <a href="/wiki/Commercialized" class="mw-redirect" title="Commercialized">commercialized</a> the production of mathematical <a href="/wiki/Theorems" class="mw-redirect" title="Theorems">theorems</a> in the same way that software companies had commercialized the production of computer programs.  He invented a number of activities and challenges of Mathematics Inc. and documented them in several papers in the EWD series. The imaginary company had produced a proof of the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_Hypothesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Riemann Hypothesis">Riemann Hypothesis</a> but then had great difficulties collecting <a href="/wiki/Royalties" class="mw-redirect" title="Royalties">royalties</a> from mathematicians who had proved results assuming the Riemann Hypothesis. The proof itself was a <a href="/wiki/Trade_secret" title="Trade secret">trade secret</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cite_EWD&#124;475_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_EWD|475-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Many of the company's proofs were rushed out the door and then much of the company's effort had to be spent on <a href="/wiki/Software_maintenance" title="Software maintenance">maintenance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> A more successful effort was the Standard Proof for <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras%27_Theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Pythagoras&#39; Theorem">Pythagoras' Theorem</a>, that replaced the more than 100 incompatible existing proofs.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Dijkstra described Mathematics Inc. as "the most exciting and most miserable business ever conceived".<sup id="cite_ref-Cite_EWD&#124;475_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cite_EWD|475-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> EWD 443 (1974) describes his fictional company as having over 75 percent of the world's market share.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="EWD_manuscripts">EWD manuscripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: EWD manuscripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dijkstra was well known for his habit of carefully composing manuscripts with his <a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">fountain pen</a>. The manuscripts are called EWDs, since Dijkstra numbered them with <i>EWD</i>, his initials, as a prefix. According to Dijkstra himself, the EWDs started when he moved from the Mathematical Centre in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> to the Eindhoven University of Technology (then Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven). After going to <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a>, Dijkstra experienced a <a href="/wiki/Writer%27s_block" title="Writer&#39;s block">writer's block</a> for more than a year. Dijkstra distributed photocopies of a new EWD among his colleagues. Many recipients photocopied and forwarded their copies, so the EWDs spread throughout the international computer science community. The topics were computer science and mathematics, and included trip reports, letters, and speeches. These short articles span a period of 40 years. Almost all EWDs appearing after 1972 were hand-written. They are rarely longer than 15 pages and are consecutively numbered. The last one, No. 1318, is from 14 April 2002. Within computer science they are known as the EWD reports, or, simply the EWDs. More than 1300 EWDs have been scanned, with a growing number transcribed to facilitate search, and are available online at the Dijkstra archive of the University of Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life_and_death">Personal life and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Personal life and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dijkstra led a modest <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyle</a>, to the point of being spartan.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> His and his wife's house in <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a> was simple, small and unassuming. He did not own a TV, a VCR or a mobile telephone, and did not go to the movies.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002_16-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt,_Krzysztof_R._2002-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He played the piano, and, while in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a>, liked to go to <a href="/wiki/Concert" title="Concert">concerts</a>. An enthusiastic listener of <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a>, Dijkstra's favorite composer was <a href="/wiki/Mozart" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozart">Mozart</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dijkstra died on 6 August 2002. According to officials at the University of Texas, the cause of death was cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_and_recognition">Influence and recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Influence and recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote" style="float:right; width:33%;"><p>The difference between a computer programmer and a computer scientist is a job-title thing. Edsger Dijkstra wants proudly to be called a "computer programmer," although he hasn't touched a computer now for some years. (...) His great strength is that he is uncompromising. It would make him physically ill to think of programming in C++.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Donald_Knuth" title="Donald Knuth">Donald Knuth</a> (1996), an interview with Donald Knuth by Jack Woehr of <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Dobb%27s_Journal" title="Dr. Dobb&#39;s Journal">Dr. Dobb's Journal</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In 1972 the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">Association for Computing Machinery</a> (ACM) acknowledged Dijkstra's seminal contributions to the field by awarding him the distinguished Turing Award. The citation for the award reads:<sup id="cite_ref-DijkstraACMtribute_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraACMtribute-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Edsger Dijkstra was a principal contributor in the late 1950s to the development of the <a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL</a>, a high level programming language which has become a model of clarity and mathematical rigor. He is one of the principal exponents of the science and art of <a href="/wiki/Programming_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming languages">programming languages</a> in general, and has greatly contributed to our understanding of their structure, representation, and implementation. His fifteen years of publications extend from theoretical articles on <a href="/wiki/Graph_theory" title="Graph theory">graph theory</a> to basic manuals, expository texts, and philosophical contemplations in the field of programming languages.</p></blockquote>
<p>The introduction given at the awards ceremony is a tribute to Dijkstra:<sup id="cite_ref-DijkstraACMtribute_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraACMtribute-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The working vocabulary of programmers everywhere is studded with words originated or forcefully promulgated by E.W. Dijkstra – display, <a href="/wiki/Deadly_embrace" class="mw-redirect" title="Deadly embrace">deadly embrace</a>, semaphore, go-to-less programming, <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a>. But his influence on programming is more pervasive than any glossary can possibly indicate. The precious gift that this Turing Award acknowledges is Dijkstra's style: his approach to programming as a high, intellectual challenge; his eloquent insistence and practical demonstration that programs should be composed correctly, not just debugged into <a href="/wiki/Correctness_(computer_science)" title="Correctness (computer science)">correctness</a>; and his illuminating perception of problems at the foundations of <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">program design</a>. (…) We have come to value good programs in much the same way as we value good literature. And at the center of this movement, creating and reflecting patterns no less beautiful than useful, stands E.W. Dijkstra.</p></blockquote>
<p>In the words of Sir <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Tony Hoare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society#Fellow" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>, delivered by him at Dijkstra's funeral:<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Edsger is widely recognized as a man who has thought deeply about many deep questions; and among the deepest questions is that of traditional moral philosophy: How is it that a person should live their life? Edsger found his answer to this question early in his life: He decided he would live as an academic scientist, conducting research into a new branch of science, the science of computing. He would lay the foundations that would establish computing as a rigorous scientific discipline; and in his research and in his teaching and in his writing, he would pursue perfection to the exclusion of all other concerns. From these commitments he never deviated, and that is how he has made to his chosen subject of study the greatest contribution that any one person could make in any one lifetime.</p></blockquote>
<p>In March 2003, the following email was sent to the <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> community:<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>This is to announce that the award formerly known as the "<a href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential-Paper Award</a>" has been renamed the "<a href="/wiki/Edsger_W._Dijkstra_Prize_in_Distributed_Computing" class="mw-redirect" title="Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing">Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing</a>" after the late Edsger W. Dijkstra, a pioneer in the area of distributed computing. His foundational work on concurrency primitives (such as the semaphore), concurrency problems (such as <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a>), reasoning about concurrent systems, and <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a> comprises one of the most important supports upon which the field of distributed computing is built. No other individual has had a larger influence on research in principles of distributed computing.</p></blockquote>
<p>Former <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">ACM</a> President <a href="/wiki/Peter_J._Denning" title="Peter J. Denning">Peter J. Denning</a> wrote about Dijkstra:<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Edsger Dijkstra, one of the giants of our field and a passionate believer in the mathematical view of programs and programming (...) Over the previous quarter-century, he had formulated many of the great intellectual challenges of the field as programming—the goto statement, structured programming, concurrent processes, semaphores, deadlocks, recursive programming in Algol, and deriving correct programs.</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Among Dijkstra's awards and honors are:<sup id="cite_ref-UTx_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UTx-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences">Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> (1971)<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Distinguished Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/British_Computer_Society" title="British Computer Society">British Computer Society</a> (1971)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">Association for Computing Machinery</a>'s <a href="/wiki/A.M._Turing_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="A.M. Turing Award">A.M. Turing Award</a> (1972)<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harry_H._Goode_Memorial_Award" title="Harry H. Goode Memorial Award">Harry H. Goode Memorial Award</a> from the <a href="/wiki/IEEE_Computer_Society" title="IEEE Computer Society">IEEE Computer Society</a> (1974).<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Foreign Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> (1975)</li>
<li>Doctor of Science <a href="/wiki/Honoris_Causa" class="mw-redirect" title="Honoris Causa">Honoris Causa</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University_Belfast" title="Queen&#39;s University Belfast">Queen's University Belfast</a> (1976)</li>
<li>Computer Pioneer Charter Recipient from the <a href="/wiki/IEEE_Computer_Society" title="IEEE Computer Society">IEEE Computer Society</a> (1982)</li>
<li>ACM/<a href="/wiki/SIGCSE" title="SIGCSE">SIGCSE</a> Award for Outstanding Contributions to Computer Science Education (1989)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/ACM_Fellow" title="ACM Fellow">Fellow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">Association for Computing Machinery</a> (1994)<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Honorary doctorate from the <a href="/wiki/Athens_University_of_Economics_%26_Business" class="mw-redirect" title="Athens University of Economics &amp; Business">Athens University of Economics &amp; Business</a>, Greece (2001).</li></ul>
<p>In 1969, the <a href="/wiki/British_Computer_Society" title="British Computer Society">British Computer Society</a> (BCS) received approval for an award and fellowship, <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Fellow_of_the_British_Computer_Society" title="Distinguished Fellow of the British Computer Society">Distinguished Fellow of the British Computer Society</a> (DFBCS), to be awarded under <a href="/wiki/Bylaw" class="mw-redirect" title="Bylaw">bylaw</a> 7 of their <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">royal charter</a>. In 1971, the first election was made, to Dijkstra.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1990, on occasion of Dijkstra's 60th birthday, the Department of Computer Science (UTCS) at the University of Texas at Austin organized a two-day seminar in his honor. Speakers came from all over the United States and Europe, and a group of computer scientists contributed research articles which were edited into a book.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_14-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeijenGries1990_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeijenGries1990-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2002, the C&amp;C Foundation of Japan recognized Dijkstra "for his pioneering contributions to the establishment of the scientific basis for computer software through creative research in basic software theory, algorithm theory, structured programming, and semaphores." Dijkstra was alive to receive notice of the award, but it was accepted by his family in an award ceremony after his death.
</p><p>Shortly before his death in 2002, Dijkstra received the ACM <a href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential-Paper Award</a> in <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> for his work on self-stabilization of program computation. This annual award was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra_Prize" title="Dijkstra Prize">Dijkstra Prize</a> (<a href="/wiki/Edsger_W._Dijkstra_Prize_in_Distributed_Computing" class="mw-redirect" title="Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing">Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing</a>) the following year, in his honor.
</p><p>The Dijkstra Award for Outstanding Academic Achievement in Computer Science (<a href="/wiki/Loyola_University_Chicago" title="Loyola University Chicago">Loyola University Chicago</a>, Department of Computer Science) is named for Edsger W. Dijkstra. Beginning in 2005, this award recognizes the top academic performance by a graduating computer science major. Selection is based on GPA in all major courses and election by department faculty.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Department of Computer Science (UTCS) at the University of Texas at Austin hosted the inaugural Edsger W. Dijkstra Memorial Lecture on 12 October 2010. <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Tony Hoare</a>, Emeritus Professor at Oxford and Principal Researcher at Microsoft Research, was the speaker for the event. This lecture series was made possible by a generous grant from Schlumberger to honor the memory of Dijkstra.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Selected_publications">Selected publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Selected publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl><dt>Books</dt></dl>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1004355016">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns dl,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ol,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin" style="">
<ul><li><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r999302996">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Lock-green.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .id-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-registration a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-subscription a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration{color:#555}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription span,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration span{border-bottom:1px dotted;cursor:help}.mw-parser-output .cs1-ws-icon a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg")right 0.1em center/12px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:none;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-maint{display:none;color:#33aa33;margin-left:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .citation .mw-selflink{font-weight:inherit}</style><cite class="citation book cs1">&#8212; (1962). <i>A Primer of ALGOL 60 Programming: Together with Report on the Algorithmic Language ALGOL 60</i>. Academic Press. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0122162503" title="Special:BookSources/978-0122162503"><bdi>978-0122162503</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Primer+of+ALGOL+60+Programming%3A+Together+with+Report+on+the+Algorithmic+Language+ALGOL+60&amp;rft.pub=Academic+Press&amp;rft.date=1962&amp;rft.isbn=978-0122162503&amp;rft.aulast=Dijkstra&amp;rft.aufirst=Edsger+W.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra" class="Z3988"></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"/><cite id="CITEREFHoare2010" class="citation web cs1"><a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Hoare, C.A.R.</a> (12 October 2010). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.cs.utexas.edu/users/EWD/DijkstraMemorialLectures/Tony%20Hoare.html">"The 2010 Edsger W. Dijkstra Memorial Lecture: What Can We Learn from Edsger W. Dijkstra?"</a>. Department of Computer Science, The University of Texas at Austin<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">12 August</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+2010+Edsger+W.+Dijkstra+Memorial+Lecture%3A+What+Can+We+Learn+from+Edsger+W.+Dijkstra%3F&amp;rft.pub=Department+of+Computer+Science%2C+The+University+of+Texas+at+Austin&amp;rft.date=2010-10-12&amp;rft.aulast=Hoare&amp;rft.aufirst=C.A.R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cs.utexas.edu%2Fusers%2FEWD%2FDijkstraMemorialLectures%2FTony%2520Hoare.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Wirth2005-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2005_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2005_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2005_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2005_2-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Niklaus_Wirth" title="Niklaus Wirth">Niklaus Wirth</a> (2005): "Of great influence to <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a> was <a href="/wiki/Structured_Programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured Programming">Structured Programming</a>, put forth by E. W. Dijkstra. This method of proceeding in a design would obliviously be greatly encouraged by the use of a <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">Structured Language</a>, a language with a set of constructs that could freely be combined and nested. The textual structure of a program should directly reflect its flow of control."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Wirth2008-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wirth2008_3-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"/><cite id="CITEREFWirth2008" class="citation journal cs1"><a href="/wiki/Niklaus_Wirth" title="Niklaus Wirth">Wirth, Niklaus</a> (July–September 2008). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://people.inf.ethz.ch/wirth/Miscellaneous/IEEE-Annals.pdf">"A Brief History of Software Engineering"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>IEEE Annals of the History of Computing</i>. <b>30</b> (3): 32–39. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1109%2FMAHC.2008.33">10.1109/MAHC.2008.33</a>. <a href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:9035976">9035976</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">22 September</span> 2020</span>. <q>In 1965 Dijkstra wrote his famous <i>Notes on Structured Programming</i> and declared programming as a discipline in contrast to a craft. Also in 1965 <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Hoare</a> published an important paper about data structuring. These ideas had a profound influence on new programming languages, in particular <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>. Languages are the vehicles in which these ideas were to be expressed. Structured programming became supported by a <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured programming language</a>.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=IEEE+Annals+of+the+History+of+Computing&amp;rft.atitle=A+Brief+History+of+Software+Engineering&amp;rft.volume=30&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=32-39&amp;rft.date=2008-07%2F2008-09&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1109%2FMAHC.2008.33&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A9035976%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.aulast=Wirth&amp;rft.aufirst=Niklaus&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fpeople.inf.ethz.ch%2Fwirth%2FMiscellaneous%2FIEEE-Annals.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his 2004 memoir, "A Programmer's Story: The Life of a Computer Pioneer", Brinch Hansen wrote that he used "Cooperating Sequential Processes" to guide his work implementing multiprogramming on the RC 4000, and described it saying, "One of the great works in computer programming, this masterpiece laid the conceptual foundation for concurrent programming."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-LLamport2002-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-LLamport2002_5-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"/><cite id="CITEREFLamport2002" class="citation web cs1">Lamport, Leslie (2002). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.podc.org/influential/2002-influential-paper/">"2002 PODC Influential Paper Award"</a>. ACM Symposium on Principles of Distributed Computing<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">22 September</span> 2020</span>. <q>Edsger W. Dijkstra started the field of concurrent and distributed algorithms with his 1965 CACM paper "Solution of a Problem in Concurrent Programming Control", in which he first stated and solved the mutual exclusion problem. That paper is probably why <a href="/wiki/PODC" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC">PODC</a> exists; it certainly inspired most of my work.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=2002+PODC+Influential+Paper+Award&amp;rft.pub=ACM+Symposium+on+Principles+of+Distributed+Computing&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.aulast=Lamport&amp;rft.aufirst=Leslie&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.podc.org%2Finfluential%2F2002-influential-paper%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEdsger+W.+Dijkstra" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Lamport_(2015)-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamport_(2015)_6-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLamport2015">Lamport (2015)</a></span>

<li id="cite_note-Dolev2000-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Dolev2000_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dolev2000_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Shlomi_Dolev" title="Shlomi Dolev">Dolev, Shlomi</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">Self-stabilization</a></i>.
